    <meta name="dc.title" content="An improved draft genome sequence of hybrid Populus alba &#8201;&#215;&#8201; Populus glandulosa"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Journal of Forestry Research 2020"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Springer"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2020-10-19"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2020 Northeast Forestry University"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Populus alba&#8201;&#215;&#8201;P. glandulosa clone 84&#160;K, derived from South Korea, is widely cultivated in China and used as a model in the molecular research of woody plants because of high gene transformation efficiency. Here, we combined 63-fold coverage Illumina short reads and 126-fold coverage PacBio long reads to assemble the genome. Due to the high heterozygosity level at 2.1% estimated by k-mer analysis, we exploited TrioCanu for genome assembly. The PacBio clean subreads of P. alba&#8201;&#215;&#8201;P. glandulosa were separated into two parts according to the similarities, compared with the parental genomes of P. alba and P. glandulosa. The two parts of the subreads were assembled to two sets of subgenomes comprising subgenome A (405.31&#160;Mb, from P. alba) and subgenome G (376.05&#160;Mb, from P. glandulosa) with the contig N50 size of 5.43&#160;Mb and 2.15&#160;Mb, respectively. A high-quality P. alba&#8201;&#215;&#8201;P. glandulosa genome assembly was obtained. The genome size was 781.36&#160;Mb with the contig N50 size of 3.66&#160;Mb and the longest contig was 19.47&#160;Mb. In addition, a total of 176.95&#160;Mb (43.7%), 152.37&#160;Mb (40.5%) of repetitive elements were identified and a total of 38,701 and 38,449 protein-coding genes were predicted in subgenomes A and G, respectively. For functional annotation, 96.98% of subgenome A and 96.96% of subgenome G genes were annotated with public databases. This de novo assembled genome will facilitate systematic and comprehensive study, such as multi-omics analysis, in the model tree P. alba&#8201;&#215;&#8201;P. glandulosa."/>

        <meta property="og:url" content="https://link.springer.com/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2"/>
        <meta property="og:type" content="article"/>
        <meta property="og:site_name" content="Journal of Forestry Research"/>
        <meta property="og:title" content="An improved draft genome sequence of hybrid Populus alba  ×  Populus glandulosa"/>
        <meta property="og:description" content="Populus alba × P. glandulosa clone 84&amp;nbsp;K, derived from South Korea, is widely cultivated in China and used as a model in the molecular research of woody plants because of high gene transformation efficiency. Here, we combined 63-fold coverage Illumina short reads and 126-fold coverage PacBio long reads to assemble the genome. Due to the high heterozygosity level at 2.1% estimated by k-mer analysis, we exploited TrioCanu for genome assembly. The PacBio clean subreads of P. alba × P. glandulosa were separated into two parts according to the similarities, compared with the parental genomes of P. alba and P. glandulosa. The two parts of the subreads were assembled to two sets of subgenomes comprising subgenome A (405.31&amp;nbsp;Mb, from P. alba) and subgenome G (376.05&amp;nbsp;Mb, from P. glandulosa) with the contig N50 size of 5.43&amp;nbsp;Mb and 2.15&amp;nbsp;Mb, respectively. A high-quality P. alba × P. glandulosa genome assembly was obtained. The genome size was 781.36&amp;nbsp;Mb with the contig N50 size of 3.66&amp;nbsp;Mb and the longest contig was 19.47&amp;nbsp;Mb. In addition, a total of 176.95&amp;nbsp;Mb (43.7%), 152.37&amp;nbsp;Mb (40.5%) of repetitive elements were identified and a total of 38,701 and 38,449 protein-coding genes were predicted in subgenomes A and G, respectively. For functional annotation, 96.98% of subgenome A and 96.96% of subgenome G genes were annotated with public databases. This de novo assembled genome will facilitate systematic and comprehensive study, such as multi-omics analysis, in the model tree P. alba × P. glandulosa."/>
        <meta property="og:image" content="https://media.springernature.com/w200/springer-static/cover/journal/11676.jpg"/>

        function OptanonWrapper() {
            var elementInside = function(candidate, element) {
                if (candidate === element) {
                    return true;
                } else if (candidate.nodeName.toLowerCase() === 'body') {
                    return false;
                } else {
                    return elementInside(candidate.parentNode, element);
                }
            };

            var disclaimer = document.querySelector('.c-disclaimer[aria-hidden="false"]');
            window.dataLayer.push({event:'OneTrustGroupsUpdated'});
            if (disclaimer) {
                if (!elementInside(document.activeElement, disclaimer)) {
                    disclaimer.querySelector('button').focus();
                }
            } else {
                document.activeElement.blur();
            }
        }

                <div data-article-body="true" data-track-component="article body" class="c-article-body">
                    <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p><i>Populus alba</i> × <i>P. glandulosa</i> clone 84 K, derived from South Korea, is widely cultivated in China and used as a model in the molecular research of woody plants because of high gene transformation efficiency. Here, we combined 63-fold coverage Illumina short reads and 126-fold coverage PacBio long reads to assemble the genome. Due to the high heterozygosity level at 2.1% estimated by <i>k</i>-mer analysis, we exploited TrioCanu for genome assembly. The PacBio clean subreads of <i>P. alba</i> × <i>P. glandulosa</i> were separated into two parts according to the similarities, compared with the parental genomes of <i>P. alba</i> and <i>P. glandulosa</i>. The two parts of the subreads were assembled to two sets of subgenomes comprising subgenome A (405.31 Mb, from <i>P. alba</i>) and subgenome G (376.05 Mb, from <i>P. glandulosa</i>) with the contig N50 size of 5.43 Mb and 2.15 Mb, respectively. A high-quality <i>P. alba</i> × <i>P. glandulosa</i> genome assembly was obtained. The genome size was 781.36 Mb with the contig N50 size of 3.66 Mb and the longest contig was 19.47 Mb. In addition, a total of 176.95 Mb (43.7%), 152.37 Mb (40.5%) of repetitive elements were identified and a total of 38,701 and 38,449 protein-coding genes were predicted in subgenomes A and G, respectively. For functional annotation, 96.98% of subgenome A and 96.96% of subgenome G genes were annotated with public databases. This de novo assembled genome will facilitate systematic and comprehensive study, such as multi-omics analysis, in the model tree <i>P. alba</i> × <i>P. glandulosa</i>.</p></div></div></section>
                    
    


                    

                    

                    
                        
                            <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Forests play important roles in maintaining ecological equilibrium on earth. <i>Populus</i> (poplar) is one of the major tree genera cultivated widely for both ecological and economic objectives. The genus has been used as a model in research because of rapid growth, small genome, and successful transformation system (Bradshaw et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2000" title="Bradshaw HD, Davis CJ, Stettler R (2000) Emerging model systems in plant biology: poplar (Populus) as a model forest tree. J Plant Growth Regulation 19(3):306–313" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d39074e575">2000</a>). Currently, system biology with multi-omics integration has been applied to poplar to identify key factors controlling important traits and to construct genetic regulator networks, especially for wood formation (Yuan et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2008" title="Yuan JS, Galbraith DW, Dai SY, Griffin P, Stewart CN (2008) Plant systems biology comes of age. Trends Plant Sci 13(4):165–171" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d39074e578">2008</a>; Moreno-Risueno et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2010" title="Moreno-Risueno MA, Busch W, Benfey PN (2010) Omics meet networks—using systems approaches to infer regulatory networks in plants. Curr Opin Plant Biol 13(2):126–131" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d39074e581">2010</a>; Li et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2014" title="Li Q, Song J, Peng S, Wang JP, Qu G-Z, Sederoff RR, Chiang VL (2014) Plant biotechnology for lignocellulosic biofuel production. Plant Biotechnol J 12(9):1174–1192" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d39074e584">2014</a>). The efficient integration of genomics and other omics relies on the quality of genome sequences. Since first completion of genome sequencing of the species <i>P. trichocarpa</i> (Torr. and Gray) (Tuskan et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2006" title="Tuskan GA, DiFazio S, Jansson S, Bohlmann J, Grigoriev I, Hellsten U, Putnam N, Ralph S, Rombauts S, Salamov A, Schein J, Sterck L, Aerts A, Bhalerao RR, Blaudez D, Boerjan W, Brun A, Brunner A, Busov V, Campbell M, Carlson J, Chalot M, Chapman J, Chen GL, Cooper D, Coutinho PM, Couturier J, Covert S, Cronk Q, Cunningham R, Davis J, Degroeve S, Déjardin A, DePamphilis C, Detter J, Dirks B, Dubchak I, Duplessis S, Ehlting J, Ellis B, Gendler K, Goodstein D, Gribskov M, Grimwood J, Groover A, Gunter L, Hamberger B, Heinze B, Helariutta Y, Henrissat B, Holligan D, Holt R, Huang W, Islam-Faridi N, Jones S, Jones-Rhoades M, Jorgensen R, Joshi C, Kangasjärvi J, Karlsson J, Kelleher C, Kirkpatrick R, Kirst M, Kohler A, Kalluri U, Larimer F, Leebens-Mack J, Leplé JC, Locascio P, Lou Y, Lucas S, Martin F, Montanini B, Napoli C, Nelson DR, Nelson C, Nieminen K, Nilsson O, Pereda V, Peter G, Philippe R, Pilate G, Poliakov A, Razumovskaya J, Richardson P, Rinaldi C, Ritland K, Rouzé P, Ryaboy D, Schmutz J, Schrader J, Segerman B, Shin H, Siddiqui A, Sterky F, Terry A, Tsai CJ, Uberbacher E, Unneberg P, Vahala J, Wall K, Wessler S, Yang G, Yin T, Douglas C, Marra M, Sandberg G, Van de Peer Y, Rokhsar D (2006) The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science 313:1596–1604" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d39074e591">2006</a>), high quality genome sequences have been assembled for white poplar, <i>P. alba</i> L. and its var. <i>phramidalis</i> (Liu et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2019" title="Liu Y-J, Wang X-R, Zeng Q-Y (2019) De novo assembly of white poplar genome and genetic diversity of white poplar population in Irtysh River basin in China. Sci China Life Sci 62(5):609–618" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d39074e600">2019</a>; Ma et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2019" title="Ma J, Wan D, Duan B, Bai X, Bai Q, Chen N, Ma T (2019) Genome sequence and genetic transformation of a widely distributed and cultivated poplar. Plant Biotechnol J 17(2):451–460" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d39074e603">2019</a>) and in other two species <i>P. euphratica</i> Oliv. and <i>P. pruinosa</i> Schrenk that grow in deserts (Ma et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2013" title="Ma T, Wang J, Zhou G, Yue Z, Hu Q, Chen Y, Liu B, Qiu Q, Wang Z, Zhang J, Wang K, Jiang D, Guo C, Yu L, Zhan D, Zhou R, Luo W, Ma H, Yang Y, Pan S, Fang D, Luo Y, Wang X, Wang G, Wang J, Wang Q, Lu X, Chen Z, Liu J, Lu Y, Yin Y, Yang H, Abbott RJ, Wu Y, Wan D, Li J, Yin T, Lascoux M, DiFazio SP, Tuskan GA, Wang J, Liu J (2013) Genomic insights into salt adaptation in a desert poplar. Nat Commun 4:2797" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d39074e613">2013</a>; Yang et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2017" title="Yang W, Wang K, Zhang J, Ma J, Liu J, Ma T (2017) The draft genome sequence of a desert tree Populus pruinosa. Gigascience 6(9):1–7" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d39074e616">2017</a>). Although a transformation system in <i>P. trichocarpa</i> has been established (Song et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2006" title="Song J, Lu S, Chen Z-Z, Lourenco R, Chiang VL (2006) Genetic transformation of Populus trichocarpa genotype Nisqually-1: a functional genomic tool for woody plants. Plant Cell Physiol 47(11):1582–1589" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d39074e622">2006</a>; Li et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2017" title="Li S, Zhen C, Xu W, Wang C, Cheng Y (2017) Simple, rapid and efficient transformation of genotype Nisqually-1: a basic tool for the first sequenced model tree. Sci Rep 7(1):2638" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d39074e626">2017</a>), it is still not successful in some laboratories. This species can only grow in some regions, limiting its spread for research. Hybrid poplar, <i>P. alba</i> × <i>P. glandulosa</i> cl. 84 K, widely cultivated in China and Korea, is used as a model to study tree growth and disease resistance (Zhang et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2011" title="Zhang B, Chen M, Zhang X, Luan H, Diao S, Tian Y, Su X (2011) Laboratory and field evaluation of the transgenic Populus alba × Populus glandulosa expressing double coleopteran-resistance genes. Tree Physiol 31(5):567–573" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d39074e635">2011</a>; Ke et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2017" title="Ke QB, Kim HS, Wang Z, Ji CY, Jeong JC, Lee HS, Choi YI, Xu B, Deng X, Yun DJ, Kwak SS (2017) Down-regulation of GIGANTEA-like genes increases plant growth and salt stress tolerance in poplar. Plant Biotechnol J 15(3):331–343" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d39074e638">2017</a>; Yoon et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2018" title="Yoon SK, Bae Ek, Lee H, Choi YI, Han M, Choi H, Kang KS (2018) Downregulation of stress-associated protein 1 (PagSAP1) increases salt stress tolerance in poplar (Populus alba × P. glandulosa). Trees 32(3):823–833" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d39074e641">2018</a>). A high transformation efficiency has been achieved using leaves as explants (Li et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2017" title="Li S, Zhen C, Xu W, Wang C, Cheng Y (2017) Simple, rapid and efficient transformation of genotype Nisqually-1: a basic tool for the first sequenced model tree. Sci Rep 7(1):2638" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d39074e645">2017</a>), attracting more research laboratories to use this hybrid. Moreover, the improved genome sequence of <i>P. alba</i> × <i>P. glandulosa</i> will provide important reference for future research and it will facilitate systematic and comprehensive studies, such as multi-omics analysis, molecular breeding practices, and comparative genomic analysis with other species and hybrids.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Materials and methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Materials and methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Plant materials, DNA and RNA extraction</h3><p>Fresh young leaves and stem differentiating xylem were collected from a single 1-year-old <i>P. alba</i> × <i>P. glandulosa</i> plant in the YuQuanShan garden of the Chinese Academy of Forestry in Beijing and immediately frozen in liquid nitrogen. Young leaves of <i>P. glandulosa</i> were also sampled for DNA extraction using the modified CTAB (hexadecyl trimethyl ammonium bromide) method (Porebski et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1997" title="Porebski S, Bailey LG, Baum BR (1997) Modification of a CTAB DNA extraction protocol for plants containing high polysaccharide and polyphenol components. Plant Mol Biol Rep 15(1):8–15" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d39074e675">1997</a>). In addition, tissues of leaves, stem xylem and phloem of <i>P. alba</i> × <i>P. glandulosa</i> were collected from three individual trees and used for total RNA isolation using the CTAB method (Chang et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1993" title="Chang S, Puryear J, Cairney J (1993) A simple and efficient method for isolating RNA from pine trees. Plant Mol Biol Rep 11(2):113–116" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d39074e685">1993</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Library construction and sequencing</h3><p>Both xylem and leaf DNA from the hybrid were used for PacBio (Pacific Biosciences, Memlo Park, CA, USA) single-molecule real-time (SMRT) sequencing because the xylem tissues contain fewer chloroplasts compared to leaves. Libraries were constructed using ~ 20 kilobases (kb) size DNA fragments following the manual of PacBio. A total of 58 SMRT cells were sequenced, of which 50 from xylem DNA libraries were run on the PacBio RS II platform; six cells from xylem DNA libraries and two cells from leaf DNA libraries were run on the PacBio Sequel platform. A total of 59.72 gigabases (Gb) subreads with an N50 length of 8151 base pair (bp) were generated. After removing adaptors, correcting sequences and selecting the subreads of more than 5000 bp, 46.67 Gb of clean subreads were obtained and used for the de novo genome assembly (Table S1).</p><p>In addition, three Illumina-shorted DNA libraries with an insert size of ~ 350 bp were constructed for <i>P. alba</i> × <i>P. glandulosa</i> using the TruSeq DNA Sample Prep Kit (Illumina Inc., San Diego, CA, USA). Libraries were pair-end sequenced with 150 bp both sides on the Illumina HiSeq X-10 platform, resulting in a total of 30 Gb raw data. The raw reads were subjected to a quality check and filtered by fastp (<a href="https://github.com/OpenGene/fastp">https://github.com/OpenGene/fastp</a>). The raw reads were processed by removing adapter sequences, low-quality reads and possible contaminated reads. In total, 27.6 Gb of clean data were obtained (Table S1). An Illumina-shorted DNA library with insert size of ~ 550 bp was constructed for <i>P. glandulosa</i> using Truseq Nano DNA Prep Kit. The library was pair-end sequenced with 151 bp both sides on the Illumina Novaseq 6000 platform, resulting in a total of 46.5 Gb raw data (Table S2).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Transcriptome sequencing</h3><p>After quality checking on an Agilent Bioanalyzer 2100, total RNA of leaves, phloem and xylem, each with three biological replicates from three individual trees, were used for RNA-seq library construction using the NEB Next Ultra Directional RNA Library Prep Kit for Illumina (Ispawich, NE, USA) following the manufacturer’s instructions. The nine libraries were sequenced using the Illumina HiSeq 4000 platform with a read length of 150 bp at both sides. Raw data with 25.6 Gb, 23.9 Gb and 25.7 Gb were obtained for leaves, phloem and xylem, respectively. More than 57 million paired-end reads were generated for each sample (Table S3). These RNA-seq reads were then assembled using Trinity (Grabherr et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2011" title="Grabherr MG, Haas BJ, Moran Y, Levin JZ, Thompson DA, Ido A, Xian A, Fan L, Raktima R, Zeng Q, Chen Z, Evan M, Nir H, Andreas G, Nicholas R, Palma Fd, Birren BW, Chad N, Kerstin L, Nir F, Aviv R (2011) Full-length transcriptome assembly from RNA-Seq data without a reference genome. Nat Biotechnol 29(7):644–652" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d39074e723">2011</a>) with the default parameters, and a total of 103,140 (&gt; 300 bp) fragments were obtained for subsequent transcriptome-based gene prediction. All sequencing reads for genome and transcriptome have been deposited in the NCBI Sequence Read Archive (SRA) under BioProject number PRJNA526157.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Estimation of genome size and heterozygosity</h3><p>To guide genome assembly, the genome size of <i>P. alba</i> × <i>P. glandulosa</i> was determined by 17-mer frequency distribution analysis with KMC (Marek et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2017" title="Marek K, Maciej D, Sebastian D (2017) KMC 3: counting and manipulating k-mer statistics. Bioinformatics 33(17):2759–2761" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d39074e740">2017</a>) and GenomeScope (Vurture et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2017" title="Vurture GW, Sedlazeck FJ, Nattestad M, Underwood CJ, Fang H, Gurtowski J, Schatz MC (2017) GenomeScope: fast reference-free genome profiling from short reads. Bioinformatics 33(14):2202–2204" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d39074e743">2017</a>) using the 27.6 Gb Illumina sequencing reads. The final result was plotted as a frequency graph (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#Fig1">1</a>). The genome size was estimated to be 474.43 Mb. The two distinctive peaks observed from the distribution curve demonstrate a high heterozygosity of the <i>P. alba</i> × <i>P. glandulosa</i> genome, with an estimated heterozygosity level of 2.1% (Table S4), indicating a 97.9% sequence identity between parental haplotypes.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs11676-020-01235-2/MediaObjects/11676_2020_1235_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1007%2Fs11676-020-01235-2/MediaObjects/11676_2020_1235_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="596" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>17-mer analysis for <i>P. alba</i> × <i>P. glandulosa</i> genome; x-axis is the <i>k</i>-mer depth, the y-axis is the frequency of the <i>k</i>-mer for a given depth</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Genome assembly</h3><p>The higher heterozygosity level increases the difficulty of genome assembly. Xu et al. (<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2020" title="Xu Z, Pu X, Gao R, Demurtas CD, Fleck SJ, Richter M, He C, Ji A, Sun W, Kong J, Hu K, Ren F, Song J, Wang Z, Gao T, Xiong C, Yu H, Xin T, Albert VA, Giuliano G, Chen S, Song J (2020) Tandem gene duplications drive divergent evolution of caffeine and crocin biosynthetic pathways in plants. BMC Biol 18(1):63" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d39074e795">2020</a>) used Canu (Koren et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2017" title="Koren S, Walenz BP, Berlin K, Miller JR, Bergman NH, Phillippy AM (2017) Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res 27(5):722–736" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d39074e798">2017</a>) to assemble the genome of <i>Gardenia jasminoides</i> J.Ellis, which had a comparable level of heterozygosity as <i>P. alba</i> × <i>P. glandulosa.</i> We conducted genome assembly using the TrioCanu (Koren et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2018" title="Koren S, Rhie A, Walenz BP, Dilthey AT, Bickhart DM, Kingan SB, Hiendleder S, Williams JL, Smith TPL, Phillippy AM (2018) De novo assembly of haplotype-resolved genomes with trio binning. Nat Biotechnol 36:1174–1182" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d39074e811">2018</a>) software, a newly developed de novo sequence assembler especially for the assembly of haplotype-resolved sequences from diploid genomes. The genome sequence of <i>P. alba</i> (Liu et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2019" title="Liu Y-J, Wang X-R, Zeng Q-Y (2019) De novo assembly of white poplar genome and genetic diversity of white poplar population in Irtysh River basin in China. Sci China Life Sci 62(5):609–618" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d39074e817">2019</a>) was downloaded from NCBI (Accession number: PRJNA491245) and used as a template to simulate 45 Gb Illumina reads for haplotype phasing by pirs (<a href="https://github.com/galaxy001/pirs">https://github.com/galaxy001/pirs</a>). We also obtained 46.5 Gb Illumina reads for <i>P. glandulosa</i>. The Illumina sequencing reads from the two parental genomes were used to partition the long reads from an offspring into haplotype-specific sets, and each haplotype was assembled independently, resulting in a complete diploid reconstruction. The separated PacBio reads were corrected, trimmed and assembled into contigs using Canu v2.0 (Koren et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2017" title="Koren S, Walenz BP, Berlin K, Miller JR, Bergman NH, Phillippy AM (2017) Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res 27(5):722–736" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d39074e830">2017</a>) with the default parameters<b>.</b> The resultant contigs were polished with Racon (Vaser et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2017" title="Vaser R, Sovic I, Nagarajan N, Sikic M (2017) Fast and accurate de novo genome assembly from long uncorrected reads. Genome Res 27(5):737–746" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d39074e837">2017</a>) independently.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Repeat annotation</h3><p>Repetitive sequences and transposable elements (TEs) in the two sets of subgenomes (A and G) were respectively identified and classified using a combination of de novo and homology-based approaches at DNA level. Initially, a de novo repeat library using RepeatModeler (Tarailo-Graovac and Chen <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2009" title="Tarailo Graovac M, Chen N (2009) Using RepeatMasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Curr Protocols Bioinformatics 25:4.10.1–4.10.14" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d39074e848">2009</a>) was built with default parameters. RepeatMasker (<a href="https://www.repeatmasker.org/">https://www.repeatmasker.org/</a>) was then conducted to map the assemblies against both the databases that we had constructed and the known Repbase (Jurka et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2005" title="Jurka J, Kapitonov VV, Pavlicek A, Klonowski P, Kohany O, Walichiewicz J (2005) Repbase update, a database of eukaryotic repetitive elements. Cytogenetic Genome Res 110:462–467" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d39074e858">2005</a>) TE library. In this process, the Dfam (Hubley et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2016" title="Hubley R, Finn RD, Clements J, Eddy SR, Jones TA, Bao W, Smit AFA, Wheeler TJ (2016) The Dfam database of repetitive DNA families. Nucleic Acids Res 44(D1):D81–D89" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d39074e861">2016</a>) database version is 3.0 and the Repbase version is 20,181,026.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Gene annotation</h3><p>We exploited MAKER3 pipeline (Cantarel et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2008" title="Cantarel BL, Korf I, Robb SMC, Parra G, Ross E, Moore B, Holt C, Alvarado AS, Yandell M (2008) MAKER: an easy-to-use annotation pipeline designed for emerging model organism genomes. Genome Res 18(1):188–196" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d39074e872">2008</a>) which required sufficient evidence from a combination of protein, EST and ab initio gene prediction input. For homology-based prediction, annotated proteins from five sequenced plants (<i>P. trichocarpa</i>, <i>Ricinus communis</i>, <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Salix suchowensis</i> and <i>Vitis vinifera</i>) were independently mapped to the subgenomes A and G using TBLASTN (Camacho et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2009" title="Camacho C, George C, Vahram A, Ning M, Jason P, Kevin B, Thomas LM (2009) BLAST+: architecture and applications. BMC Bioinformatics 10:421" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d39074e891">2009</a>) with default parameters. The <i>P. alba</i> × <i>P. glandulosa</i> transcriptome fragments based on Inchworm assembly were used as the EST evidence in MAKER3 pipeline. For ab initio gene prediction, the results from the above RepeatMasker analysis were used as the reference in the repeat sequences analysis, with eukaryotic selected in the organism type. We adopted the three methods SNAP (Korf <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2004" title="Korf I (2004) Gene finding in novel genomes. BMC Bioinformatics 5(1):59" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d39074e900">2004</a>), GeneMark (Alexandre et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2005" title="Alexandre L, Vardges TH, Chernoff YO, Mark B (2005) Gene identification in novel eukaryotic genomes by self-training algorithm. Nucleic Acids Res 33(20):6494–6506" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d39074e903">2005</a>) and Augustus (Stanke and Waack <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2003" title="Stanke M, Waack S (2003) Gene prediction with a hidden Markov model and a new intron submodel. Bioinformatics 19(suppl_2):ii215-ii225" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d39074e907">2003</a>) for de novo annotation for the repetitive sequences masked genome.</p><p>Gene function annotation was performed with several protein databases (SwissProt, KEGG, BIGG, and COG) to predict protein domains for functional information. In addition, Gene Ontology (GO) annotation was performed by AHRD (<a href="https://github.com/groupschoof/AHRD">https://github.com/groupschoof/AHRD</a>), which is an informative select description and GO terms tool. SwissProt (Brigitte et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2003" title="Brigitte B, Amos B, Rolf A, Marie-Claude B, Anne E, Elisabeth G, Maria JM, Karine M, Claire O, Isabelle P, Sandrine P, Michel S (2003) The SWISS-PROT protein knowledgebase and its supplement TrEMBL in 2003. Nucleic Acids Res 31(1):365–370" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d39074e920">2003</a>) was selected as the database for protein queries, and the weights were assigned by default.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Comparative genome analysis with <i>P. trichocarpa</i>
</h3><p>The number of protein-coding genes and the results of BUSCO analysis with the same parameters were compared between <i>P. alba</i> × <i>P. glandulosa</i> genome and <i>P. trichocarpa</i> v4.1 genome. Gene similarity analysis was also carried out to compare protein-coding genes between these two. We independently conducted genome structure analysis between subgenome A and <i>P. trichocarpa</i>, and between subgenome G and <i>P. trichocarpa</i> using MCScanX software (Wang et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2012" title="Wang Y, Tang H, Debarry JD, Tan X, Li J, Wang X, Lee TH, Jin H, Marler B, Guo H, Kissinger JC, Paterson AH (2012) MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res 40(7):e49" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d39074e951">2012</a>) and genome sequences as inputs. Some monolignol biosynthetic pathway enzyme genes in subgenomes A and G were identified through BioEdit (Alzohairy <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2011" title="Alzohairy AM (2011) BioEdit: an important software for molecular biology. Gerf Bull Biosci 2:60–61" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d39074e954">2011</a>), and their gene structures were compared with paralogous genes in <i>P. trichocarpa</i> using the GSDS 2.0 pipeline (Hu et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2015" title="Hu B, Jin J, Guo A-Y, Zhang H, Luo J, Gao G (2015) GSDS 2.0: an upgraded gene feature visualization server. Bioinformatics 31:1296–1297" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d39074e960">2015</a>).</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Genome assembly and assessment</h3><p>Consequently, two sets of subgenomes were assembled, comprising subgenome A (405.31 Mb from <i>P. alba</i>) and subgenome G (376.05 Mb from <i>P. glandulosa</i>) with the contig N50 size of 5.43 Mb and 2.15 Mb, respectively. By merging the sequences of two subgenomes together, we obtained a high-quality <i>P. alba</i> × <i>P. glandulosa</i> genome assembly with a total length of 781.36 Mb containing 2,109 contigs (N50 = 3.66 Mb; N90 = 168.21 Kb) and the longest contig was 19.47 Mb (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#Tab1">1</a>). The distribution of the average guanylic and cytidylic acid (GC) content of the <i>P. alba</i> × <i>P. glandulosa</i> genome was 34.1%, similar to that in <i>P. euphratica</i> (32.1%) (Ma et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2013" title="Ma T, Wang J, Zhou G, Yue Z, Hu Q, Chen Y, Liu B, Qiu Q, Wang Z, Zhang J, Wang K, Jiang D, Guo C, Yu L, Zhan D, Zhou R, Luo W, Ma H, Yang Y, Pan S, Fang D, Luo Y, Wang X, Wang G, Wang J, Wang Q, Lu X, Chen Z, Liu J, Lu Y, Yin Y, Yang H, Abbott RJ, Wu Y, Wan D, Li J, Yin T, Lascoux M, DiFazio SP, Tuskan GA, Wang J, Liu J (2013) Genomic insights into salt adaptation in a desert poplar. Nat Commun 4:2797" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d39074e1001">2013</a>) and <i>P. trichocarpa</i> (33.6%) (Tuskan et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2006" title="Tuskan GA, DiFazio S, Jansson S, Bohlmann J, Grigoriev I, Hellsten U, Putnam N, Ralph S, Rombauts S, Salamov A, Schein J, Sterck L, Aerts A, Bhalerao RR, Blaudez D, Boerjan W, Brun A, Brunner A, Busov V, Campbell M, Carlson J, Chalot M, Chapman J, Chen GL, Cooper D, Coutinho PM, Couturier J, Covert S, Cronk Q, Cunningham R, Davis J, Degroeve S, Déjardin A, DePamphilis C, Detter J, Dirks B, Dubchak I, Duplessis S, Ehlting J, Ellis B, Gendler K, Goodstein D, Gribskov M, Grimwood J, Groover A, Gunter L, Hamberger B, Heinze B, Helariutta Y, Henrissat B, Holligan D, Holt R, Huang W, Islam-Faridi N, Jones S, Jones-Rhoades M, Jorgensen R, Joshi C, Kangasjärvi J, Karlsson J, Kelleher C, Kirkpatrick R, Kirst M, Kohler A, Kalluri U, Larimer F, Leebens-Mack J, Leplé JC, Locascio P, Lou Y, Lucas S, Martin F, Montanini B, Napoli C, Nelson DR, Nelson C, Nieminen K, Nilsson O, Pereda V, Peter G, Philippe R, Pilate G, Poliakov A, Razumovskaya J, Richardson P, Rinaldi C, Ritland K, Rouzé P, Ryaboy D, Schmutz J, Schrader J, Segerman B, Shin H, Siddiqui A, Sterky F, Terry A, Tsai CJ, Uberbacher E, Unneberg P, Vahala J, Wall K, Wessler S, Yang G, Yin T, Douglas C, Marra M, Sandberg G, Van de Peer Y, Rokhsar D (2006) The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science 313:1596–1604" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d39074e1007">2006</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Comparison of the new genome with previously published assembly of the <i>P. alba</i> × <i>P. glandulosa </i>cl. 84 K at contig scale, and statistics of subgenome A and subgenome G assemblies in this study. Contig N50 refers to the size above which 50% of the total length of the sequence assembly can be found. N60, N70, N80 and N90 are similarly defined</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Three methods were carried out to evaluate the accuracy and the completeness of the <i>P. alba</i> × <i>P. glandulosa</i> genome, subgenomes A as well as G. A total of 27.6 Gb (~ 59X) Illumina short reads were aligned back to the <i>P. alba</i> × <i>P. glandulosa</i> genome, subgenome A and subgenome G using BWA (Md et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2019" title="Md V, Misra S, Li H, Aluru S (2019) Efficient architecture-aware acceleration of BWA-MEM for multicore systems. IEEE international parallel and distributed processing symposium, pp 314–324" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d39074e1380">2019</a>) with default parameters and the analysis showed that 99.4%, 98.7% and 97.8% of the reads were successfully mapped, respectively. A BUSCO (Simão et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2015" title="Simão FA, Waterhouse RM, Panagiotis I, Kriventseva EV, Zdobnov EM (2015) BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31(19):3210–3212" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d39074e1384">2015</a>) and MUMmer (Kurtz et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2004" title="Kurtz S, Phillippy A, Delcher AL, Smoot M (2004) Versatile and open software for comparing large genomes. Genome Biol 5(2):R12.1–R12.9." href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d39074e1387">2004</a>) analyses were conducted. BUSCO analysis with the recently released plant dataset from OrthoDB (<a href="https://www.orthodb.org">https://www.orthodb.org</a>) showed that 98.1%, 98.5%, 96.9% (1349, 1354, 1332 out of 1375) of the core eukaryotic genes had been recovered in the <i>P. alba</i> × <i>P. glandulosa</i> genome, subgenome A and subgenome G, respectively. A total of 2,344,256 SNPs sites and 1,460,279 short INDELs were identified, and the divergence between the parental haplotypes was estimated to be 3% with MUMmer. The RNA-seq reads generated in this study from three tissues were matched to the <i>P. alba</i> × <i>P. glandulosa</i> genome, subgenome A and subgenome G using BWA (Li <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2013" title="Li H (2013) Aligning sequence reads, clone sequences and assembly contigs with BWA-MEM. Genomics" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d39074e1410">2013</a>), showing a more than 98.0% mapping rate (Table S3). All assessments above indicated that the <i>P. alba</i> × <i>P. glandulosa</i> genome assembly was qualified for the subsequent applications.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Repeat and gene annotation</h3><p>In total, the annotation indicated approximately 43.7% (176.95 Mb) repeat sequences for subgenome A, and 40.5% (152.37 Mb) for subgenome G. Of which, long terminal repeats (LTRs) were the most abundant repeat class, accounting for 49.1% and 46.9% of repetitive sequences, representing 21.4% and 19.0% of the subgenomes A and G, respectively (Table S5). Tandem repeats were also identified using Tandem Repeat Finder (TRF) package (Gary <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1999" title="Gary B (1999) Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res 27(2):573–580" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d39074e1427">1999</a>), and the results showed a total of 169,369 and 152,982 repeats in subgenomes A and G, respectively.</p><p>After removing the unreasonable genes through the revision function of MAKER3, a total of 38,701 and 38,449 protein-coding genes in the subgenomes A and G with an average coding sequence (CDS) length of 1,122.86 and 1,112.34 bp, respectively were annotated (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#Tab2">2</a>). To assess the completeness of the two sets of subgenomes, the BUSCO analysis with the recently released plant dataset from OrthoDB was executed (Simão et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2015" title="Simão FA, Waterhouse RM, Panagiotis I, Kriventseva EV, Zdobnov EM (2015) BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31(19):3210–3212" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d39074e1436">2015</a>). The results showed that 95.0%, 93.5% (1306, 1286 out of 1375) of the core eukaryotic genes could be presented in subgenome A and G, respectively. In addition, 37,532 (97.0%) protein-coding genes in subgenome A and 37,280 (97.0%) in subgenome G can be annotated in at least one database. After the BLASTP comparison, a total of 6,979,765 and 6,924,093 hits occurred and in the process of summarizing the information for the genes, GO annotation information was obtained for 37,286 and 37,051 genes, accounting for 96.3%, 96.4% of the total number of genes for subgenomes A and G, respectively (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#Tab2">2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 Summary of genome annotation and functional annotation of <i>P. alba</i> × <i>P. glandulosa</i> comprising subgenomes A and G</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Comparative genome analysis with <i>P. trichocarpa</i>
</h3><p>Coding genes were annotated in the <i>P. alba</i> × <i>P. glandulosa</i> genome and the low-quality genes were removed. A total of 77,150 (subgenome A: 38,701; G: 38,449) coding genes were identified for the <i>P. alba</i> × <i>P. glandulosa</i> genome, comparable to the gene number (34,699) in <i>P. trichocarpa</i> v4.1 genome. The gene numbers in <i>Populus</i> genomes are generally higher than in other genomes with a median size (400–500 Mb). The <i>Populus</i> genus underwent a recent whole genome duplication event, and nearly 8000 pairs of paralogous genes of similar age, (excluding tandem or local duplications), were generated after polyploidization. Moreover, the current annotation of the <i>P. alba</i> × <i>P. glandulosa</i> genome covered 1,333 (97.0%) of the 1,375 BUSCOs and 16 (1.2%) are fragmented. The completeness of the annotated genes in <i>P. trichocarpa</i> using BUSCOs was assessed with the same parameters. Version 4.1 annotation of <i>P. trichocarpa</i> covered 1,372 (99.8%) of the 1,375 BUSCOs without fragments.</p><p>Since <i>P. trichocarpa</i> is the first poplar species with genome sequencing and its genome has been well annotated, the protein-coding genes in <i>P. alba</i> × <i>P. glandulosa</i> were compared with those in <i>P. trichocarpa</i>. A total of 61,929 (subgenome A: 31,271; G: 30,658) protein-coding genes, accounting for 80.3% in the <i>P. alba</i> × <i>P. glandulosa</i> genome, have high similarities with the genes in <i>P. trichocarpa</i>. Of which, 45,442 (subgenome A: 22,933; G: 22,509) genes have &gt; 90.0% sequence similarity with their paralogous genes in <i>P. trichocarpa</i> and 33,894 (subgenome A: 17,147; G: 16,747) genes have &gt; 95.0% sequence similarity with their paralogous genes in <i>P. trichocarpa.</i> Synteny analysis on genome structure showed that 257 blocks existed between subgenome A and <i>P. trichocarpa</i> and 253 blocks between subgenome G and <i>P. trichocarpa</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#Fig2">2</a>). We identified 28 genes encoding key enzymes in monolignol biosynthetic pathway in subgenomes A and G (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#Tab3">3</a>). Three genes with corresponding symbol names were regarded as a group. Three genes in one group had the same number of exons and introns. The lengths of three genes in most groups looked similar, such as <i>PAL1</i>, <i>CCoAOMT1</i>, <i>C4H2</i>, <i>CAld5H2</i> groups. While the lengths of three genes in several groups exhibited obvious differences, the length of intron in <i>PtrCSE1</i> was longer than in <i>Pag_A_CSE1</i> and <i>Pag_G_CSE1</i> in <i>CSE1</i> group, and the length of intron in <i>PtrCAld5H1</i> was shorter than in <i>Pag_A_CAld5H1</i> and <i>Pag_G_CAld5H1</i> in <i>CAld5H1</i> group (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#Fig3">3</a>). Whether the variations in length cause differences in enzyme activities needs further investigations.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec15" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec15-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec15">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec15-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">De novo assembler selection</h3><p>Nowadays, more and more genomes of plant species have been sequenced. At the same time, many bioinformatic tools for genome de novo assembly were exploited, such as Canu (Koren et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2017" title="Koren S, Walenz BP, Berlin K, Miller JR, Bergman NH, Phillippy AM (2017) Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res 27(5):722–736" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d39074e2608">2017</a>), Smartdenovo (Istace et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2017" title="Istace B, Friedrich A, d’Agata L, Faye S, Payen E, Beluche O, Caradec C, Davidas S, Cruaud C, Liti G, Lemainque A, Engelen S, Wincker P, Schacherer J, Aury JM (2017) De novo assembly and population genomic survey of natural yeast isolates with the Oxford Nanopore MinION sequencer. Gigascience 6(2):1–13" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d39074e2611">2017</a>) and Wtdbg2 (Ruan and Li <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2019" title="Ruan J, Li H (2019) Fast and accurate long-read assembly with wtdbg2. Nat Methods 17:155–158" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d39074e2614">2019</a>). Due to different genome ploidy and high genome heterozygosity, complex allelic variation hampers the assembly of haplotype-resolved sequences from diploid genomes. It is crucial to choose a suitable software for high quality genome assembly. Each genome assembly strategy and software used have advantages and limitations. For example, Wtdbg2 is a newly developed de novo sequence assembler for long noisy reads produced by PacBio or Oxford Nanopore Technologies. It assembles raw reads without error corrections and usually collapses heterozygosity. ALLHIC (Zhang et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2019" title="Zhang X, Zhang S, Zhao Q, Ming R, Tang H (2019) Assembly of allele-aware, chromosomal-scale autopolyploid genomes based on Hi-C data. Nat Plants 5:833–845" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d39074e2617">2019</a>) has been used for genome assembly in autopolyploid sugarcane <i>Saccharum spontaneum</i> L. It is a new Hi-C scaffolding pipeline, specifically tailored to the highly heterozygous diploids or polyploid genomes. The advent of long-read sequencing technologies has not overcome the challenge of completely assembling both haplotypes of a diploid genome. Instead, most genome assembly tools simply co-assemble the haplotypes into a mosaic consensus, resulting in an assembly that does not accurately represent either original haplotype. Collapsing haplotypes into a single consensus representation introduces false variants that are not present in either haplotype, leading to annotation and analysis errors (Korlach et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2017" title="Korlach J, Gedman G, Kingan S, Chin CS, Howard JT, Audet JN, Cantin L, Jarvis ED (2017) De novo PacBio long-read and phased avian genome assemblies correct and add to reference genes generated with intermediate and short reads. Gigascience 6:1–16" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d39074e2624">2017</a>). Ideally, a genome should be represented as a complete set of haplotypes rather than an artificial mixture. TrioCanu (Koren et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2018" title="Koren S, Rhie A, Walenz BP, Dilthey AT, Bickhart DM, Kingan SB, Hiendleder S, Williams JL, Smith TPL, Phillippy AM (2018) De novo assembly of haplotype-resolved genomes with trio binning. Nat Biotechnol 36:1174–1182" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d39074e2627">2018</a>) uses short reads from two parental genomes to first partition long reads from an offspring into haplotype-specific sets, simplifying haplotype assembly by resolving allelic variation prior to assembly. Each haplotype is then assembled independently, resulting in a complete diploid reconstruction. Here, to better avoid cross interference of genome information from parental sequences and improve assembly accuracy, we chose TrioCanu (Koren et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2018" title="Koren S, Rhie A, Walenz BP, Dilthey AT, Bickhart DM, Kingan SB, Hiendleder S, Williams JL, Smith TPL, Phillippy AM (2018) De novo assembly of haplotype-resolved genomes with trio binning. Nat Biotechnol 36:1174–1182" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d39074e2630">2018</a>) to assemble the genome of hybrid diploid <i>P. alba</i> × <i>P. glandulosa</i>, which has a high heterozygosity. This software has been used in the genome assembly of <i>Bos taurus</i> and <i>B. taurus indicus</i> and demonstrate that the quality of each haplotype exceeds that of even the best livestock reference genomes (Koren et al. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2018" title="Koren S, Rhie A, Walenz BP, Dilthey AT, Bickhart DM, Kingan SB, Hiendleder S, Williams JL, Smith TPL, Phillippy AM (2018) De novo assembly of haplotype-resolved genomes with trio binning. Nat Biotechnol 36:1174–1182" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d39074e2646">2018</a>). Choosing a suitable software is vital for genome assembly and further influences genome annotation.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Genome assembly and annotation quality</h3><p>High-quality genome assembly and annotation provide foundation for accurate downstream analysis. In this study, we assembled <i>P. alba</i> × <i>P. glandulosa</i> genome of 781.36 Mb, comprising two sets of subgenomes A and G. Compared with the genome assembly by Qiu et al. (<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2019" title="Qiu D, Bai S, Ma J, Zhang L, Shao F, Zhang K, Yang Y, Sun T, Huang J, Zhou Y, Galbraith DW, Wang Z, Sun G (2019) The genome of Populus alba x Populus tremula var. glandulosa clone 84K. DNA Res 26(5):423–431" href="/article/10.1007/s11676-020-01235-2#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d39074e2663">2019</a>), the contig N50 size of our assembly (3.66 Mb) was 1.6 times bigger at contig level. We used several methods in the gene annotation. By integrating the results from different approaches, we obtained sufficient evidence to make annotation result more accurate and complete. The BUSCO evaluations of genome assembly and annotation both showed a &gt; 97.0% coverage of the 1375 BUSCOs, and near 97.0% protein-coding genes in either subgenome A or subgenome G were functionally annotated. The protein-coding genes in <i>P. alba</i> × <i>P. glandulosa</i> were compared with those in <i>P. trichocarpa</i> and 80.3% genes had similarities with the genes in <i>P. trichocarpa</i>, exhibiting a comparative annotation level. In total, our assembly and annotation of <i>P. alba</i> × <i>P. glandulosa</i> genome can satisfy the following functional genomic analysis.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec18" data-title="Conclusions"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec18-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec18">Conclusions</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec18-content"><p><i>P. alba</i> × <i>P. glandulosa</i> is a model hybrid to study growth, wood formation and stress response<i>.</i> The flowering of <i>P. alba</i> × <i>P. glandulosa</i> has been surveyed in several locations in China over 10 years and it was found that some trees are monoecious, providing an ideal material to study sex determination in poplar. The improved assembly of <i>P. alba</i> × <i>P. glandulosa</i> is noted and the comparison of the genomes between <i>P. alba</i> × <i>P. glandulosa</i> and <i>P. trichocarpa</i> shows that this assembled genome can accelerate the study of tree growth and response to adverse environments, improving productivity and resistance to stress. The genome assembly and annotation files are available at Figshare database (<a href="https://doi.org/10.6084/m9.figshare.12369209">https://doi.org/10.6084/m9.figshare.12369209</a>).</p></div></div></section>
